<td>Gyeongbuseon</td>

<td>Kyŏngbusŏn</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b><a href="/wiki/Gyeongbu" title="Gyeongbu">Gyeongbu</a> Line</b> (<i>Gyeongbuseon</i>) is the most important <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Railway" title="Railway">railway</a> line in <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> and one of the oldest. Constructed in 1905, connecting <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a> to <a href="/wiki/Suwon" title="Suwon">Suwon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daejeon" title="Daejeon">Daejeon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daegu" title="Daegu">Daegu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Busan" title="Busan">Busan</a>. The Gyeongbu Line is by far the most heavily travelled rail line in South Korea. <a href="/wiki/Korea_Train_Express" title="Korea Train Express">High-speed</a>, express, local, and freight trains provide frequent service along its entire length.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gyeongbu_Line&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Groundbreaking_ceremony_of_Gyeongbu_Line_at_Busan,_1901.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1115" data-file-width="1048" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Groundbreaking_ceremony_of_Gyeongbu_Line_at_Busan%2C_1901.jpg/220px-Groundbreaking_ceremony_of_Gyeongbu_Line_at_Busan%2C_1901.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Groundbreaking_ceremony_of_Gyeongbu_Line_at_Busan%2C_1901.jpg/330px-Groundbreaking_ceremony_of_Gyeongbu_Line_at_Busan%2C_1901.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Groundbreaking_ceremony_of_Gyeongbu_Line_at_Busan%2C_1901.jpg/440px-Groundbreaking_ceremony_of_Gyeongbu_Line_at_Busan%2C_1901.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Groundbreaking_ceremony_of_Gyeongbu_Line_at_Busan,_1901.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Groundbreaking celebration of the railway line from Seoul to Busan in 1901</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GyeongbuTimesSpeeds.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="462" data-file-width="613" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/GyeongbuTimesSpeeds.png/220px-GyeongbuTimesSpeeds.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/GyeongbuTimesSpeeds.png/330px-GyeongbuTimesSpeeds.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/GyeongbuTimesSpeeds.png/440px-GyeongbuTimesSpeeds.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GyeongbuTimesSpeeds.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Evolution of shortest travel times and top speeds between Seoul and Busan on the Gyeongbu Line</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Gyeongbu Line was originally constructed as a vehicle of colonialism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-imperial-japan_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-imperial-japan-1">[1]</a></sup> In 1894-1895, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Japan" title="Imperial Japan">Imperial Japan</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" title="Qing Dynasty">Qing Dynasty</a> <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> fought the First Sino-Japanese War for influence over Korea. Following the war, in competition with <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>'s rail expansion across China, Japan sought the right from the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Empire" title="Korean Empire">Korean Empire</a> to build a railway from Busan to Seoul, the Gyeonbu Line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-imperial-japan_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-imperial-japan-1">[1]</a></sup> Surveying began in 1896, and in spite of local protests, the Korean Empire gave Japan the right to build the line in 1898.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-donga-hist_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-donga-hist-2">[2]</a></sup> Construction of the Gyeongbu Line started on August 20, 1901, with a ceremony at Yeongdeungpo, Seoul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-donga-hist_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-donga-hist-2">[2]</a></sup> Construction was done by locals commanded to do forced labour, and paid with coupons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-donga-hist_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-donga-hist-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan also sought to gain control of the <a href="/wiki/Gyeongui_Line" title="Gyeongui Line">Gyeongui Line</a> project that was to continue tracks further north, recognising the trunk route as a means to keep Korea under its influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-imperial-japan_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-imperial-japan-1">[1]</a></sup> The line was also advanced for military considerations in expectation of a confrontation with Russia, which came in 1904 as the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">Russo-Japanese War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-imperial-japan_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-imperial-japan-1">[1]</a></sup> At the start of the war, Japan ignored Korea's declaration of neutrality and transported troops to <a href="/wiki/Incheon" title="Incheon">Incheon</a>, and forced the Korean government to sign an agreement that gave Japan's military control of the railway. Troop bases were established in connection with the railway, the biggest of them next to <a href="/wiki/Yongsan_Station" title="Yongsan Station">Yongsan Station</a> in Seoul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-imperial-japan_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-imperial-japan-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Construction of the Gyeongbu Line was completed on January 1, 1905.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-donga-hist_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-donga-hist-2">[2]</a></sup> The first trains travelled the line in 17 hours 4 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-korail-ts_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-korail-ts-3">[3]</a></sup> By April 1906, travel time was reduced to 11 hours,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-korail-ts_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-korail-ts-3">[3]</a></sup> while top speed was 60 km/h (37 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rn-ts_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-rn-ts-4">[4]</a></sup> The line developed into the backbone of transport in <a href="/wiki/Korea_under_Japanese_rule" title="Korea under Japanese rule">Korea under Japanese rule</a>. Following the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_invasion_of_Manchuria" title="Japanese invasion of Manchuria">Japanese invasion of Manchuria</a>, from April 1, 1933, the line was traversed by direct trains from Busan to Andong (today <a href="/wiki/Dandong" title="Dandong">Dandong</a>) across the border.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kriic2_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-kriic2-5">[5]</a></sup> From December 1, 1936, the <i>Akatsuki</i> luxury express trains ran on the line with a maximum speed of 90 km/h (56 mph), and achieved the shortest pre-war travel time of 6 hours 30 minutes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-korail-ts_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-korail-ts-3">[3]</a></sup> in the timetable valid from November 1, 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rs-ts_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-rs-ts-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Travel times increased greatly while the line was used for transport in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rs-ts_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-rs-ts-6">[6]</a></sup> Following World War II, the Seoul–Busan express train re-established on May 20, 1946,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rs-ts_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-rs-ts-6">[6]</a></sup> was named <i>Chosun Liberator</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kriic2_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-kriic2-5">[5]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, the line transported troops and refugees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kriic1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-kriic1-7">[7]</a></sup> The line remained the backbone of transport in South Korea after the war,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EffectofHSR2008_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-EffectofHSR2008-8">[8]</a></sup> when <a href="/wiki/Diesel_locomotive" title="Diesel locomotive">diesel locomotives</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kriic2_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-kriic2-5">[5]</a></sup> and the cross-country <a href="/wiki/Mugunghwa-ho" title="Mugunghwa-ho">Mugunghwa-ho</a> train class was introduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-korail-ts_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-korail-ts-3">[3]</a></sup> Following the 1961 coup, the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Council_for_National_Reconstruction" title="Supreme Council for National Reconstruction">Supreme Council for National Reconstruction</a> started South Korea's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five-year_plans_of_South_Korea#1962.E2.80.931966" title="Five-year plans of South Korea">first five-year plan</a>, which included a construction program to complete the railway network, to foster economic growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> On the Gyeongbu Line, the effort was advertised with a new class of express trains named <i>Jaegeon-ho</i>, (Reconstruction train) introduced on May 15, 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kriic2_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-kriic2-5">[5]</a></sup> These trains reduced travel times below the best pre-WWII travel times for the first time, connecting Seoul and Busan in 6 hours 10 minutes at a top speed of 100 km/h (62 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-korail-ts_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-korail-ts-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>From the 1960s, road construction began to make road transport more attractive and faster. Although top speed rose to 110 km/h (68 mph) and the Seoul–Busan travel time along the Gyeongbu Line was reduced to 4 hours 50 minutes by June 10, 1969,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-korail-ts_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-korail-ts-3">[3]</a></sup> on the parallel <a href="/wiki/Gyeongbu_Expressway" title="Gyeongbu Expressway">Gyeongbu Expressway</a>, completed in 1970, travel time was only 4 hours to 4 hours 30 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EffectofHSR2008_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-EffectofHSR2008-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Korail#History" title="Korail">Korean National Railroad</a> responded by introducing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saemaul-ho" title="Saemaul-ho">Saemaul-ho</a> class of elevated-comfort express trains on August 15, 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-korail-ts_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-korail-ts-3">[3]</a></sup> with the introduction of new <a href="/wiki/Streamliner" title="Streamliner">streamlined</a> diesel locomotives and then <a href="/wiki/Diesel_multiple_unit" title="Diesel multiple unit">diesel multiple units</a> in Saemaul-ho service,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kriic2_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-kriic2-5">[5]</a></sup> top speed was raised to 140 km/h (87 mph) and travel time was reduced to 4 hours 10 minutes with the timetable valid from November 16, 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-korail-ts_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-korail-ts-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Upgrade">Upgrade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gyeongbu_Line&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Upgrade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Gyeongbu Line was extensively upgraded in parallel with the development of the <a href="/wiki/Seoul_Metropolitan_Subway" title="Seoul Metropolitan Subway">Seoul Metropolitan Subway</a> urban rapid transit system and the <a href="/wiki/Korea_Train_Express" title="Korea Train Express">Korea Train Express</a> (KTX) high-speed rail system from the 1970s.</p>
<p>The Gyeongbu Line is six-tracked from Seoul to Guro, four-tracked from Guro to Cheonan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-electrification_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-electrification-10">[10]</a></sup> and double-tracked from Cheonan all the way to Busan. The entire line is electrified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-electrification_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-electrification-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_the_KTX_project">Relationship with the KTX project</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gyeongbu_Line&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Relationship with the KTX project">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>-<a href="/wiki/Busan" title="Busan">Busan</a> axis is Korea's main traffic corridor. In 1995, it housed 73.3% of Korea's population, and conducted 70% of the freight traffic and 66% of the passenger traffic. With both the <a href="/wiki/Gyeongbu_Expressway" title="Gyeongbu Expressway">Gyeongbu Expressway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Korail" title="Korail">Korail</a>'s Gyeongbu Line congested, the government saw the need to develop railways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EffectofHSR2008_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-EffectofHSR2008-8">[8]</a></sup> The first proposals for a second Seoul-Busan railway line originated from a study prepared between 1972 and 1974 by experts of France's <a href="/wiki/SNCF" title="SNCF">SNCF</a> and Japan Railway Technical Service (JARTS) on a request from the <a href="/wiki/International_Bank_for_Reconstruction_and_Development" title="International Bank for Reconstruction and Development">IBRD</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EffectofHSR2008_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-EffectofHSR2008-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> A more detailed 1978-1981 study by <a href="/wiki/KAIST" title="KAIST">KAIST</a>, focusing on the needs of freight transport, also came to the conclusion that the necessary capacity for freight transport on the existing Gyeongbu Line could best be released by separating off long-distance passenger traffic on a parallel high speed passenger railway, which was then taken up in Korea's next <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five-year_plans_of_South_Korea" title="Five-year plans of South Korea">Five Year Plan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EffectofHSR2008_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-EffectofHSR2008-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1997_Asian_Financial_Crisis" title="1997 Asian Financial Crisis">1997 Asian Financial Crisis</a>, the government decided to finish the Gyeongbu High Speed Railway (Gyeongbu HSR) in two phases, and upgrade and electrify the conventional Gyeongbu Line for KTX services on the sections paralleling the parts of the high-speed line not completed in the first phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EffectofHSR2008_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-EffectofHSR2008-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iatss-costs_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-iatss-costs-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Plans foresaw the development of the Gyeongbu Line into a high-capacity freight corridor after the completion of the second phase of the Gyeongbu HSR.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rgi-korailplans_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-rgi-korailplans-13">[13]</a></sup> At the time of the opening of the Daegu–Busan section of the high-speed line on November 1, 2010, capacity available for freight trains on the conventional line was expected to increase by a factor of 7.7, while the capacity for passenger transport in the entire corridor increased by a factor of 3.4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrification">Electrification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gyeongbu_Line&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Electrification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>For KTX trains and new electric locomotives, top speed was also raised to up to 150 km/h.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-electrification_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-electrification-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Services">Services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gyeongbu_Line&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Gyeongbu Line is the major route out of <a href="/wiki/Seoul_Station" title="Seoul Station">Seoul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yongsan_Station" title="Yongsan Station">Yongsan Stations</a> and, in addition to regular departures for Busan, trains travel along the Gyeongbu Line en route to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Janghang&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Janghang (page does not exist)">Janghang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gwangju" title="Gwangju">Gwangju</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mokpo" title="Mokpo">Mokpo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suncheon,_South_Korea" title="Suncheon, South Korea">Suncheon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yeosu" title="Yeosu">Yeosu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pohang" title="Pohang">Pohang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ulsan" title="Ulsan">Ulsan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haeundae" title="Haeundae">Haeundae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masan_(Korea)" title="Masan (Korea)">Masan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jinju" title="Jinju">Jinju</a>. Trains for <a href="/wiki/Jecheon" title="Jecheon">Jecheon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andong" title="Andong">Andong</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yeongju" title="Yeongju">Yeongju</a> also operate along sections of the Gyeongbu Line.</p>
<p>On the section between Seoul Station, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guro-gu" title="Guro-gu">Guro</a> (where roughly half of the trains leave the Gyeongbu Line to head out to <a href="/wiki/Incheon" title="Incheon">Incheon</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Gyeongin_Line" title="Gyeongin Line">Gyeongin Line</a>), Suwon, and Byeongjeom, <a href="/wiki/Seoul_Subway_Line_1" title="Seoul Subway Line 1">Seoul Subway Line 1</a> provides frequent commuter services.</p>
<p>The Gyeongbu Line is served along its entire length by frequent intercity <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saemaul-ho" title="Saemaul-ho">Saemaul-ho</a> and cross-country <a href="/wiki/Mugunghwa-ho" title="Mugunghwa-ho">Mugunghwa-ho</a> trains. Some trains run along the entire length of the line, others only on some sections, including trains diverging to the connected lines. As of October 2010, direct Saemaul day trains connect Seoul to Busan in a minimum 4 hours 50 minutes, and Mughungwa trains in a minimum 5 hours 28 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-booking_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-booking-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="KTX">KTX</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gyeongbu_Line&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: KTX">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gyeongbu_High_Speed_Railway" title="Gyeongbu High Speed Railway">Gyeongbu High Speed Railway</a></div>
<p>Korail launched KTX high-speed services with the opening of the first phase of the Gyeongbu HSR on April 1, 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EffectofHSR2008_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-EffectofHSR2008-8">[8]</a></sup> The Seoul–Busan travel distance was shortened to 408.5 km, the shortest travel time was 2 hours 40 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EffectofHSR2008_8-7"><a href="#cite_note-EffectofHSR2008-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>All KTX services use the conventional Gyeongbu Line between Seoul and the start of the Siheung Interconnection at a junction after <a href="/wiki/Geumcheon-gu_Office_Station" title="Geumcheon-gu Office Station">Geumcheon-gu Office Station</a>, until the Siheung Interconnection diverges in a tunnel towards the present start of the Gyeongbu HSR. The terminal for most Gyeongbu KTX services is Seoul Station, for most <a href="/wiki/Honam_Line#Honam_KTX" title="Honam Line">Honam KTX</a> services, Yongsan Station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EffectofHSR2008_8-8"><a href="#cite_note-EffectofHSR2008-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-booking_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-booking-16">[16]</a></sup> In addition, some trains continue beyond Seoul station for 14.9 km along the <a href="/wiki/Gyeongui_Line" title="Gyeongui Line">Gyeongui Line</a> to terminate at <a href="/wiki/Haengsin_Station" title="Haengsin Station">Haengsin Station</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-booking_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-booking-16">[16]</a></sup> next to which KTX trains have a depot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaunchHSR2007_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaunchHSR2007-17">[17]</a></sup> An additional stop at <a href="/wiki/Yeongdeungpo_Station" title="Yeongdeungpo Station">Yeongdeungpo Station</a> was proposed in 2004, however, the plans were dropped in face of opposition from locals living around <a href="/wiki/Gwangmyeong_Station" title="Gwangmyeong Station">Gwangmyeong Station</a> along the Gyeongbu HSR, who feared that Yeongdeungpo would draw away passengers from the new station and force its closing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> However, the November 1, 2010, timetable change made Yeongdeungpo a KTX stop, for newly introduced trains that also use the Gyeongbu Line on the entire Seoul–Daejeon section, to serve <a href="/wiki/Suwon_Station" title="Suwon Station">Suwon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-booking_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-booking-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korail-Nov2010_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Korail-Nov2010-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>From its opening, the Gyeongbu KTX service also returned to the Gyeongbu Line for two short sections crossing Daejeon and Daegu, where local disputes about the high-speed line alignment across urban areas held up construction;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plans2020_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-plans2020-20">[20]</a></sup> and all the way from Daegu to Busan. Consequently, all but two of the stations of the Gyeongbu KTX service were on the conventional Gyeongbu Line: after the two stations on the high-speed line, Gwangmyeong and Cheonan-Asan, stops were at Daejeon, Dongdaegu (East Daegu), Miryang, Gupo and Busan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EffectofHSR2008_8-9"><a href="#cite_note-EffectofHSR2008-8">[8]</a></sup> Some Gyeongbu KTX services maintained service on this relation after the November 1, 2010, opening of the second phase of the Gyeongbu HSR, with the daily number of halts in Miryang and Gupo increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-booking_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-booking-16">[16]</a></sup> Korail met local demands by introducing additional KTX services between Seoul and Dongdaegu in June 2007, which used the conventional Gyeongbu Line between Daejeon and Dongdaegu to serve <a href="/wiki/Gimcheon" title="Gimcheon">Gimcheon</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gumi,_South_Korea" title="Gumi, South Korea">Gumi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GinGum_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-GinGum-21">[21]</a></sup> However, these services were discontinued with the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Gimcheon%E2%80%93Gumi_Station" title="Gimcheon–Gumi Station">Gimcheon–Gumi Station</a> on the high-speed line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GinGum_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-GinGum-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The section between Daegu and Samnangjin, the junction with the <a href="/wiki/Gyeongjeon_Line" title="Gyeongjeon Line">Gyeongjeon Line</a>, is also used by the Gyeongjeon KTX services, which connect Seoul to <a href="/wiki/Masan" title="Masan">Masan</a> on the Gyeongjeon Line since December 15, 2010,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and will be extended to Jinju by 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suseoannounce_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-suseoannounce-23">[23]</a></sup> Stops along the Gyeongbu Line will be at Dongdaegu and Miryang.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_of_long-distance_passenger_traffic">Evolution of long-distance passenger traffic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gyeongbu_Line&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Evolution of long-distance passenger traffic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Between Seoul and Cheonan, the Mugunghwa and Saemaul express trains on the Gyeongbu Line gave rail around a fifth of the <a href="/wiki/Modal_share" title="Modal share">modal share</a> before the launch of KTX services. Due to the short distance and the location of the KTX station outside the city, the conventional line could retain most of its passengers, and the increase in the total modal share of rail was modest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EffectofHSR2008_8-10"><a href="#cite_note-EffectofHSR2008-8">[8]</a></sup> On the medium-distance relation from Seoul to Daejeon, KTX gained market share mostly at the expense of normal express services on the Gyeongbu Line, which decreased by half in the first year, while the total share of rail increased to a third.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EffectofHSR2008_8-11"><a href="#cite_note-EffectofHSR2008-8">[8]</a></sup> On the long-distance relations from Seoul to Daegu and Busan, the total share of rail increased from around two-fifths to a market dominating three-fifths, with the bulk of that traffic taken by the KTX. For intercity passenger traffic on the conventional Gyeongbu Line, that translates to a sharp drop on the Daejeon-Daegu section (bypassed by KTX trains) and a sharp increase on the Daegu-Busan section.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EffectofHSR2008_8-12"><a href="#cite_note-EffectofHSR2008-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
